{"code":1,"isDone":false,"toInsertArticleList":[{"createdTime":"2020-11-18 00:31:14","updatedTime":"2020-11-18 00:31:14","title":"5 questions from Airbnb’s IPO filing","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2074631","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\"><a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/airbnb\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"airbnb\">Airbnb <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> filed to go public yesterday, offering the world a look into its financial performance over the past several years. <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/11/16/airbnb-files-to-go-public/\">The company&#8217;s S-1</a> detailed an expanding travel giant with billions in annual revenue that was severely disrupted by the COVID-19 pandemic.</p>\n<p>But past our overview of Airbnb&#8217;s <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/11/16/airbnb-files-to-go-public/\">core financial results</a> and our look into <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/11/16/the-vc-and-founder-winners-in-airbnbs-ipo/\">which investors will make the most</a> from its public debut, there are still questions that need answering.</p>\n<hr />\n<p><strong><em>The Exchange explores startups, markets and money. Read it <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/subscribe/?tpcc=theexchange\">every morning on Extra Crunch</a>, or get <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/newsletters\">The Exchange newsletter</a> every Saturday.</em></strong></p>\n<hr />\n<p>We need to to better understand how far Airbnb&#8217;s bookings fell during the end of Q1 and the start of Q2, when travel first collapsed. And, how far those numbers have come back since. We also want to understand what sort of booking activity is <em>driving </em>those gains &#8212; is Airbnb really benefiting from a surge of long-term, local stays?</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/subscribe/?tpcc=theexchange\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"alignright wp-image-1995210 size-medium\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/exchange-banner-sq-blu.jpg?w=300\" alt=\"\" width=\"300\" height=\"300\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/exchange-banner-sq-blu.jpg 900w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/exchange-banner-sq-blu.jpg?resize=150,150 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/exchange-banner-sq-blu.jpg?resize=300,300 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/exchange-banner-sq-blu.jpg?resize=768,768 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/exchange-banner-sq-blu.jpg?resize=680,680 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/exchange-banner-sq-blu.jpg?resize=32,32 32w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/exchange-banner-sq-blu.jpg?resize=50,50 50w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/exchange-banner-sq-blu.jpg?resize=64,64 64w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/exchange-banner-sq-blu.jpg?resize=96,96 96w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/exchange-banner-sq-blu.jpg?resize=128,128 128w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px\" /></a>Then, to close, how profitable was Airbnb when times were good, and what sort of cash stockpile does the company have to get <em>back</em> to its former scale?</p>\n<p>These five questions should help us better understand how Airbnb managed to survive some tough months and still file to go public before 2020 ran out. Let&#8217;s get to work!</p>\n<p>We&#8217;ll take each question individually, to make our homework today as simple as possible.</p>\n<h2>How far did Airbnb&#8217;s bookings fall during Q1 and Q2?</h2>\n<p>Let&#8217;s start by looking at Airbnb&#8217;s gross bookings on a quarterly basis. The company defines gross bookings as &#8220;net of cancellations and alterations,&#8221; so these numbers are not artificially inflated.</p>\n<p>Here&#8217;s the chart:</p>\n<p><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-2075001\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-8.32.12-AM.png?w=680\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"227\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-8.32.12-AM.png 700w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-8.32.12-AM.png?resize=150,50 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-8.32.12-AM.png?resize=300,100 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-8.32.12-AM.png?resize=680,227 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-8.32.12-AM.png?resize=50,17 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" /></p>\n<p>Where does the decline begin? Q1, as we&#8217;ll see when we dig into monthly data, but the above chart does a good job painting just how bad things got for Airbnb in growth terms as Q1 closed and Q2 kicked off.</p>\n<p>As you can see, Airbnb&#8217;s second quarter gross bookings were its lowest in recent history; Q2 2020 put up the smallest bookings result since at <em>least</em> the first quarter of 2017. For a company that had done $10.0 billion in gross bookings in a single quarter just over a year before, the declines were catastrophic.</p>\n<p>But the results are actually worse than that chart shows; Airbnb actually saw gross bookings go <em>negative</em> for a few months.</p>\n<p>How is that possible? Recall that the gross bookings figure <em>discounts</em> cancellations and alterations. So, if Airbnb had a big wave of cancellations, its gross bookings number could fall so sharply it goes negative, even if the company were still seeing some new bookings.</p>\n<p>That&#8217;s what happened in March and April. Observe:</p>\n<p><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-2075005\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-8.36.16-AM.png?w=680\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"159\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-8.36.16-AM.png 1006w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-8.36.16-AM.png?resize=150,35 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-8.36.16-AM.png?resize=300,70 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-8.36.16-AM.png?resize=768,179 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-8.36.16-AM.png?resize=680,159 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-8.36.16-AM.png?resize=50,12 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" /></p>\n<p>So how far did Airbnb&#8217;s gross bookings fall? They fell to -$900 million in March. More simply, Airbnb saw its expected rental volume fall by nearly $1 billion in a single month. And then in April it fell by another $600 million as more cancellations piled up.</p>\n<p>That&#8217;s why Airbnb cut staff and took on expensive capital; its business had gone from accreting to bleeding in no time at all.</p>\n<h2>How far have Airbnb&#8217;s bookings come back since?<div class=\"extra-crunch-offer-container\"></div><div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a Alliance was down nearly 10% before market open and CVS Health slid 7%.</p>\n<p>Amazon has been steadily encroaching on pharmacy businesses in the same way the company has moved into grocery delivery and everyday consumer staples.</p>\n<p>The convergence of food and pharmacy has been a decades-long evolution for mega-retailers on both sides of the divide &#8212; with grocers building out pharmacy services and pharmacies adding food to their shelves.</p>\n<p>Since its acquisition of Pillpack in 2018, Amazon has been adding additional pharmaceutical and healthcare services. It launched its own <a href=\"https://www.investopedia.com/news/amazon-launches-its-own-line-overthecounter-drugs/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">over-the-counter drugs in 2019</a>, and rolled out a healthcare network for its employees &#8212; Amazon Care for its workers in Seattle.</p>\n<p>In August, Amazon <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/08/27/amazon-debuts-halo-smart-health-subscription-service-and-halo-band-wearable-activity-tracker/\">launched its fitness tracker, Halo</a>. The personal health and wellness monitoring and advice service includes a $64.99 wrist tracker and an application suite for monitoring health.</p>\n<p>As TechCrunch noted, the service includes more than the standard health tracking gadget/app combo, by taking a comprehensive look at various measures of health, including body fat percentage, as measured at home with just your smartphone’s own camera and the Amazon Halo app.</p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"ZBksaBHJKF\"><p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/08/27/amazon-debuts-halo-smart-health-subscription-service-and-halo-band-wearable-activity-tracker/\">Amazon debuts Halo smart health subscription service and Halo Band wearable activity tracker</a></p></blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" title=\"&#8220;Amazon debuts Halo smart health subscription service and Halo Band wearable activity tracker&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/08/27/amazon-debuts-halo-smart-health-subscription-service-and-halo-band-wearable-activity-tracker/embed/#?secret=ZBksaBHJKF\" data-secret=\"ZBksaBHJKF\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe></div>\n<p>Taken together, Amazon&#8217;s array of hardware, software, pharmacy services and healthcare network represents the most complete package of health services across industries.</p>\n<p>It&#8217;s a powerful pitch to consumers, and one that could ultimately significantly drive down healthcare costs. 00:31:14","updatedTime":"2020-11-18 00:31:14","title":"YouTube targets music fans with new audio ad format","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2075125","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\"><a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/youtube\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"youtube\">YouTube <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> is announcing new ad products today, designed to help marketers reach YouTube visitors who are doing more listening than watching.</p> \n<p>The big addition is audio advertising. As the <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/google\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"google\">Google <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a>-owned video site puts it in <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2075125/ttps://blog.google/products/ads-commerce/youtube-music-audio-ads\">a blog post</a>, these are ads designed for viewers who “squeeze in a living room workout before dinner, catch up on a podcast or listen to a virtual concert on a Friday night.”</p> \n<p>In other words, audio ads are designed for videos where audience members may only be glancing at the screen occasionally, or might ignoring the visuals altogether. To be clear, these ads won’t be audio-only, but YouTube says the audio should be doing most of the communication, while the visual side is limited to “a still image or simple animation.”</p>\n<div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div> \n<p>The company says that in early testing, more than 75% of audio ad campaigns on YouTube resulted in a significant lift in brand awareness. For example, <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Fq905lMJHuU&amp;feature=youtu.be\">this Shutterfly ad</a> resulted in a 14% lift in ad recall and a 2% increase in favorability in its target audience.</p> \n<p>The key, YouTube says, is that the audio has to carry the message: “Think: If I close my eyes, I can still clearly understand what this ad is about.”</p> \n<p><iframe class=\"youtube-player\" width=\"640\" height=\"360\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/Fq905lMJHuU?version=3&amp;rel=1&amp;showsearch=0&amp;showinfo=1&amp;iv_load_policy=1&amp;fs=1&amp;hl=en-US&amp;autohide=2&amp;wmode=transparent\" allowfullscreen=\"true\" style=\"border:0;\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts allow-same-origin allow-popups allow-presentation\"></iframe></p> \n<p>In addition to launching audio ads in beta, YouTube is also announcing <a href=\"https://support.google.com/google-ads/answer/10057354\">dynamic music lineups</a>, allowing marketers to target their campaigns at collections of music channels on YouTube. These lineups can be focused on a genre, such as Latin music or K-pop, or on an interest like fitness.</p> \n<p>In <a href=\"https://www.thinkwithgoogle.com/marketing-strategies/video/music-industry-changes\">a separate blog post</a>, YouTube’s Head of Music Lyor Cohen made a broader case to advertisers about why they should see YouTube as an essential music streaming platform.</p> \n<p>After all, according to Cohen, more than 2 billion logged-in viewers are watching at least one music video each month. And, he wrote, “music is more front and center than you might think” — 60% of YouTube’s music viewing happens on mobile, where background viewing/listening is disabled.</p> \n<p>That might seem like an odd thing to emphasize while launching an ad format better suited to background listening, but Cohen continued, “Regardless of when and how people are tuning in, we have ways to help advertisers connect, even when they’re consuming music in the background. Now you can complement the moments your consumers are watching, by engaging them in moments when they’re listening, with newly announced audio ads.”</p> \n<div class=\"embed breakout\"> \n <blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"qe7C35XYD1\">\n  <p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/05/07/youtube-ads-on-tv/\">YouTube brings more ad formats and measurement to TVs</a></p>\n \n</div>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Techcrunch/~4/6bkJ5u6AKFs\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Tue, 17 Nov 2020 16:04:57 +0000","feedId":26,"bgimg":"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=2mJPEYqXBVI","linkMd5":"28890eaedac3c2e581859b2c2770a15e","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn35@2020_4/2020/11/17/16-31-24-043_cda007d67cf2c798.webp","destWidth":59,"destHeight":24,"sourceBytes":990,"destBytes":324,"author":"Anthony Ha","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=2mJPEYqXBVI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn35@2020_4/2020/11/17/16-31-24-043_cda007d67cf2c798.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=7Q72WNTAKBA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn56@2020_6/2020/11/17/16-31-36-787_6be3d6b3141e6636.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn38@2020_5/2020/11/17/16-31-34-660_ae8500a395ae4c74.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=6bkJ5u6AKFs:EV_o-P_QB7E:-BTjWOF_DHI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn63@2020_6/2020/11/17/16-31-35-804_f9de9acd132f923f.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=6bkJ5u6AKFs:EV_o-P_QB7E:D7DqB2pKExk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn91@2020_6/2020/11/17/16-31-37-107_353be3ea28f4c3f7.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn16@2020_6/2020/11/17/16-31-37-422_749e2568e12b832a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Techcrunch/~4/6bkJ5u6AKFs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn36@2020_1/2020/11/17/16-31-36-951_d7be792fe3d8d0ab.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1605630674924},{"createdTime":"2020-11-18 00:31:14","updatedTime":"2020-11-18 00:31:14","title":"Yeah, Apple’s M1 MacBook Pro is powerful, but it’s the battery life that will blow you away","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2074982","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Survival and strategy games are often played in stages. You have the early game where you’re learning the ropes, understanding systems. Then you have mid-game where you’re executing and gathering resources. The most fun part, for me, has always been the late mid-game where you’re in full control of your powers and skills and you’ve got resources to burn &#8212; where you execute on your master plan before the endgame gets hairy.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">This is where Apple is in the game of power being played by the chip industry. And it’s about to be endgame for Intel. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Apple has introduced three machines that use its new M1 system on a chip, based on over a decade’s worth of work designing its own processing units based on the ARM instructions set. These machines are capable, assured and powerful, but their greatest advancements come in the performance per watt category.</span></p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">I personally tested the 13” M1 MacBook Pro and after extensive testing, it’s clear that this machine eclipses some of the most powerful Mac portables ever made in performance while simultaneously delivering 2x-3x the battery life </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">at a minimum.</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">These results are astounding, but they’re the product of that long early game that Apple has played with the A-series processors. Beginning in earnest in 2008 with the acquisition of PA Semiconductor, Apple has been working its way towards unraveling the features and capabilities of its devices from the product roadmaps of processor manufacturers.  </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The M1 MacBook Pro runs smoothly, launching apps so quickly that they’re often open before your cursor leaves your dock. </span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/m1-dock.gif\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout size-full wp-image-2074937\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/m1-dock.gif\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"128\" /></a></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Video editing and rendering is super performant, only falling behind older machines when it leverages the GPU heavily. And even then only with powerful dedicated cards like the 5500M or VEGA II. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Compiling projects like WebKit produce better build times than nearly any machine (hell the M1 Mac Mini beats the Mac Pro by a few seconds). And it does it while using a fraction of the power. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">This thing works like an iPad. That’s the best way I can describe it succinctly. One illustration I have been using to describe what this will feel like to a user of current MacBooks is that of chronic pain. If you’ve ever dealt with ongoing pain from a condition or injury, and then had it be alleviated by medication, therapy or surgery, you know how the sudden relief feels. You’ve been carrying the load so long you didn’t know how heavy it was. That’s what moving to this M1 MacBook feels like after using other Macs. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Every click is more responsive. Every interaction is immediate. It feels like an iOS device in all the best ways. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">At the chip level, it also </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">is </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">an iOS device. Which brings us to&#8230;</span></p>\n<p><b>iOS on M1</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The iOS experience on the M1 machines is&#8230;present. That’s the kindest thing I can say about it. Apps install from the App Store and run smoothly, without incident. Benchmarks run on iOS apps show that they perform natively with no overhead. I even ran an iOS-based graphics benchmark which showed just fine. </span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-5.31.44-AM.jpeg\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2075007\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-5.31.44-AM.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"640\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-5.31.44-AM.jpeg 2880w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-5.31.44-AM.jpeg?resize=150,94 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-5.31.44-AM.jpeg?resize=300,188 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-5.31.44-AM.jpeg?resize=768,480 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-5.31.44-AM.jpeg?resize=680,425 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-5.31.44-AM.jpeg?resize=1536,960 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-5.31.44-AM.jpeg?resize=2048,1280 2048w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-5.31.44-AM.jpeg?resize=50,31 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">That, however, is where the compliments end. The current iOS app experience on an M1 machine running Big Sur is almost comical; it&#8217;s so silly. There is no default tool-tip that explains how to replicate common iOS interactions like swipe-from-edge &#8212; instead a badly formatted cheat sheet is buried in a menu. The apps launch and run in windows only. Yes, that’s right, no full-screen iOS apps at all. It’s super cool for a second to have instant native support for iOS on the Mac, but at the end of the day this is a </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">marketing</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> win, not a </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">consumer experience</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> win. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Apple gets to say that the Mac now supports millions of iOS apps, but the fact is that the experience of using those apps on the M1 is sub-par. It will get better, I have no doubt. But the app experience on the M1 is pretty firmly in this order right now: Native M1 app&#62;Rosetta 2 app&#62;Catalyst app&#62; iOS app. Provided that the Catalyst ports can be bothered to build in Mac-centric behaviors and interactions, of course. But it’s clear that iOS, though present, is clearly not where it needs to be on M1.</span></p>\n<p><b>Rosetta 2</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">There is both a lot to say and not a lot to say about Rosetta 2. I’m sure we’ll get more detailed breakdowns of how Apple achieved what it has with this new emulation layer that makes x86 applications run fine on the M1 architecture. But the real nut of it is that it has managed to make a chip so powerful that it can take the approximate 26% hit (see the following charts) in raw power to translate apps and still make them run just as fast if not </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">faster</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> than MacBooks with Intel processors. </span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-arm2-2.jpg\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2075083\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-arm2-2.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"576\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-arm2-2.jpg 3200w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-arm2-2.jpg?resize=150,84 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-arm2-2.jpg?resize=300,169 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-arm2-2.jpg?resize=768,432 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-arm2-2.jpg?resize=680,383 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-arm2-2.jpg?resize=1536,864 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-arm2-2.jpg?resize=2048,1152 2048w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-arm2-2.jpg?resize=50,28 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-intel-rosetta.jpg\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2074944\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-intel-rosetta.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"576\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-intel-rosetta.jpg 3200w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-intel-rosetta.jpg?resize=150,84 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-intel-rosetta.jpg?resize=300,169 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-intel-rosetta.jpg?resize=768,432 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-intel-rosetta.jpg?resize=680,383 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-intel-rosetta.jpg?resize=1536,864 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-intel-rosetta.jpg?resize=2048,1152 2048w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/geekbench-intel-rosetta.jpg?resize=50,28 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">It’s pretty astounding. Apple would like us to forget the </span><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rosetta_(software)\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">original Rosetta</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> from the PowerPC transition as much as we would all like to forget it. And I’m happy to say that this is pretty easy to do because I was unable to track any real performance hit when comparing it to older, even ‘more powerful on paper’ Macs like the 16” MacBook Pro. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">It’s just simply not a factor in most instances. And companies like Adobe and Microsoft are already hard at work bringing native M1 apps to the Mac, so the most needed productivity or creativity apps will essentially get a </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">free</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> performance bump of around 30% when they go native. But even now they’re just as fast. It’s a win-win situation. </span></p>\n<p><b>Methodology</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">My methodology  for my testing was pretty straightforward. I ran a battery of tests designed to push these laptops in ways that reflected both real world performance and tasks as well as synthetic benchmarks. I ran the benchmarks with the machines plugged in and then again on battery power to estimate constant performance as well as performance per watt. All tests were run multiple times with cooldown periods in between in order to try to achieve a solid baseline. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Here are the machines I used for testing:</span></p>\n<ul>\n<li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">2020 13” M1 MacBook Pro 8-core 16GB</span></li>\n<li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">2019 16” Macbook Pro 8-core 2.4GHz 32GB w/5500M</span></li>\n<li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">2019 13” MacBook Pro 4-core 2.8GHz 16GB</span></li>\n<li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">2019 Mac Pro 12-Core 3.3GHz 48GB w/AMD Radeon Pro Vega II 32GB</span></li>\n</ul>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Many of these benchmarks also include numbers from <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/11/17/the-new-mac-mini-the-revival-of-the-no-compromise-low-cost-mac\">the M1 Mac mini review from Matt Burns</a> and the M1 MacBook Air, tested by Brian Heater which you <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/11/17/macbook-air-m1-review-the-right-apple-silicon-mac-for-most/\">can check out here.</a></span></p>\n<p><b>Compiling WebKit</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Right up top I’m going to start off with the real ‘oh shit’ chart of this piece. I checked WebKit out from GitHub and ran a build on all of the machines with no parameters. This is the one deviation from the specs I mentioned above as my 13” had issues that I couldn’t figure out so I had some </span><a href=\"https://twitter.com/jamesthomson/status/1328090424456699904\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Internet friends help me</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">. Also thanks to <a href=\"https://twitter.com/tapbot_paul\">Paul Haddad</a> of Tapbots for guidance here. </span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/webkit-compile-time.jpg\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout size-full wp-image-2074943\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/webkit-compile-time.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"576\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/webkit-compile-time.jpg 3200w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/webkit-compile-time.jpg?resize=150,84 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/webkit-compile-time.jpg?resize=300,169 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/webkit-compile-time.jpg?resize=768,432 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/webkit-compile-time.jpg?resize=680,383 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/webkit-compile-time.jpg?resize=1536,864 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/webkit-compile-time.jpg?resize=2048,1152 2048w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/webkit-compile-time.jpg?resize=50,28 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">As you can see, the M1 performs admirably well across all models, with the MacBook and Mac Mini edging out the MacBook Air. This is a pretty straightforward way to visualize the difference in performance that can result in heavy tasks that last over 20 minutes, where the MacBook Air’s lack of active fan cooling throttles back the M1 a bit. Even with that throttling, the MacBook Air still beats everything here except for the very beefy Mac Pro. </span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/WebKit-Compile-Battery-Remaining.png\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2074995\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/WebKit-Compile-Battery-Remaining.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"476\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/WebKit-Compile-Battery-Remaining.png 1874w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/WebKit-Compile-Battery-Remaining.png?resize=150,70 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/WebKit-Compile-Battery-Remaining.png?resize=300,140 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/WebKit-Compile-Battery-Remaining.png?resize=768,357 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/WebKit-Compile-Battery-Remaining.png?resize=680,316 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/WebKit-Compile-Battery-Remaining.png?resize=1536,715 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/WebKit-Compile-Battery-Remaining.png?resize=50,23 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">But, the big deal here is really this second chart. After a single build of WebKit, the M1 MacBook Pro had a massive 91% of its battery left. I tried multiple tests here and I could have easily run a full build of WebKit 8-9 times on one charge of the M1 MacBook’s battery. In comparison, I could have gotten through about 3 on the 16” and the 13” 2020 model only had one go in it. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">This insane performance per watt of power is the M1’s secret weapon. The battery performance is simply off the chart. Even with processor-bound tasks. To give you an idea, throughout this build of WebKit the P-cluster (the power cores) hit peak pretty much every cycle while the E-cluster (the efficiency cores) maintained a steady 2GHz. These things are going at it, but they’re super power efficient.</span></p>\n<p><b>Battery Life</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">In addition to charting battery performance in some real world tests, I also ran a couple of dedicated battery tests. In some cases they ran so long I thought I had left it plugged in by mistake, it’s that good. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">I ran a mixed web browsing and web video playback script that hit a series of pages, waited for 30 seconds and then moved on to simulate browsing. The results return a pretty common sight in our tests, with the M1 outperforming the other MacBooks by just over 25%.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Mixed-Web-Browsing-and-Video.png\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2074994\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Mixed-Web-Browsing-and-Video.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"539\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Mixed-Web-Browsing-and-Video.png 1878w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Mixed-Web-Browsing-and-Video.png?resize=150,79 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Mixed-Web-Browsing-and-Video.png?resize=300,158 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Mixed-Web-Browsing-and-Video.png?resize=768,404 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Mixed-Web-Browsing-and-Video.png?resize=680,358 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Mixed-Web-Browsing-and-Video.png?resize=1536,808 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Mixed-Web-Browsing-and-Video.png?resize=50,26 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">In fullscreen 4k/60 video playback, the M1 fares even better, clocking an easy 20 hours with fixed 50% brightness. On an earlier test, I left the auto-adjust on and it crossed the 24 hour mark easily. Yeah, a full day. That&#8217;s an iOS-like milestone.</span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/4k60-Full-Screen-Video-Playback.png\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2074984\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/4k60-Full-Screen-Video-Playback.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"630\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/4k60-Full-Screen-Video-Playback.png 1876w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/4k60-Full-Screen-Video-Playback.png?resize=150,92 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/4k60-Full-Screen-Video-Playback.png?resize=300,185 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/4k60-Full-Screen-Video-Playback.png?resize=768,472 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/4k60-Full-Screen-Video-Playback.png?resize=680,418 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/4k60-Full-Screen-Video-Playback.png?resize=1536,945 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/4k60-Full-Screen-Video-Playback.png?resize=50,31 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The M1 MacBook Air does very well also, but its smaller battery means a less playback time at 16 hours. Both of them absolutely decimated the earlier models.</span></p>\n<p><b>Xcode Unzip</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">This was another developer-centric test that was requested. Once again, CPU bound, and the M1’s blew away any other system in my test group. Faster than the 8-core 16” MacBook Pro, wildly faster than the 13” MacBook Pro and yes, 2x as fast as the 2019 Mac Pro with its 3.3GHz Xeons. </span></p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2074941\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/xcode-unzip-time.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2074941\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout size-full wp-image-2074941\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/xcode-unzip-time.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"576\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/xcode-unzip-time.jpg 3200w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/xcode-unzip-time.jpg?resize=150,84 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/xcode-unzip-time.jpg?resize=300,169 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/xcode-unzip-time.jpg?resize=768,432 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/xcode-unzip-time.jpg?resize=680,383 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/xcode-unzip-time.jpg?resize=1536,864 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/xcode-unzip-time.jpg?resize=2048,1152 2048w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/xcode-unzip-time.jpg?resize=50,28 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2074941\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> TechCrunch</p></div></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">For a look at the power curve (and to show that there is no throttling of the MacBook Pro over this period (I never found any throttling over longer periods by the way) here’s the usage curve.</span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/XIP-CPU-Usage.png\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2074998\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/XIP-CPU-Usage.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"364\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/XIP-CPU-Usage.png 1872w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/XIP-CPU-Usage.png?resize=150,53 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/XIP-CPU-Usage.png?resize=300,107 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/XIP-CPU-Usage.png?resize=768,273 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/XIP-CPU-Usage.png?resize=680,242 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/XIP-CPU-Usage.png?resize=1536,546 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/XIP-CPU-Usage.png?resize=50,18 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p><b>Unified Memory and Disk Speed</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Much ado has been made of Apple including only 16GB of memory on these first M1 machines. The fact of it, however, is that I have been unable to push them hard enough yet to </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">feel</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> any effect of this due to Apple’s move to unified memory architecture. Moving RAM to the SoC means no upgradeability &#8212; you’re stuck on 16GB forever. But it </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">also</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> means massively faster access to that memory by chips on the system that need it most.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">If I was a betting man I’d say that this was an intermediate step to eliminating the concept of discrete RAM altogether. It’s possible that a future (far future, this is the play for now) version of Apple’s M-series chips could end up supplying memory to each of the various chips from a vast pool that also serves as permanent storage. For now, though, what you’ve got is a finite, but blazing fast, pool of memory shared between the CPU cores, GPU and other SoC denizens like the Secure Enclave and Neural Engine. </span></p>\n<p>If you want an indicator of how <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/apple\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"apple\">Apple <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> thinks about the tight connection loop between OS X Big Sur and the M1 processor, which it was built for and vice-versa, note that the M1 MacBook Pro&#8217;s about screen does not show clock speeds at all.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-6.51.11-AM.png\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2075094\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-6.51.11-AM.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"623\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-6.51.11-AM.png 1396w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-6.51.11-AM.png?resize=150,91 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-6.51.11-AM.png?resize=300,183 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-6.51.11-AM.png?resize=768,468 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-6.51.11-AM.png?resize=680,414 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Screen-Shot-2020-11-17-at-6.51.11-AM.png?resize=50,30 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">While running many applications simultaneously, the M1 performed extremely well. Because this new architecture is so close, with memory being a short hop away next door rather than out over a PCIe bus, swapping between applications was zero issue. Even while tasks were run in the background &#8212; beefy, data heavy tasks &#8212; the rest of the system stayed flowing.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Even when the memory pressure tab of Activity Monitor showed that OS X was using swap space, as it did from time to time, I noticed no slowdown in performance. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Though I wasn’t able to trip it up I would guess that you would have to throw a single, extremely large file at this thing to get it to show any amount of struggle. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The SSD in the M1 MacBook Pro is running on a PCIe 3.0 bus, and its write and read speeds indicate that. </span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/BlackMagic-Disk-Speed.png\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2074985\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/BlackMagic-Disk-Speed.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"630\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/BlackMagic-Disk-Speed.png 1876w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/BlackMagic-Disk-Speed.png?resize=150,92 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/BlackMagic-Disk-Speed.png?resize=300,185 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/BlackMagic-Disk-Speed.png?resize=768,472 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/BlackMagic-Disk-Speed.png?resize=680,418 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/BlackMagic-Disk-Speed.png?resize=1536,945 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/BlackMagic-Disk-Speed.png?resize=50,31 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p>&#160;</p>\n<p><b>Thunderbolt and Webcam</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The M1 MacBook Pro has two Thunderbolt controllers, one for each port. This means that you’re going to get full PCIe 3.0 x4 speeds out of each and that it seems </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">very likely</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> that Apple could include up to 4 ports in the future without much change in architecture. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">This configuration also means that you can easily power an Apple Pro Display XDR and another monitor besides. I was unable to test two Apple Pro Display XDR monitors side-by-side.</span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/iPhone-Comp-Template.jpg\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2075067\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/iPhone-Comp-Template.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"682\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/iPhone-Comp-Template.jpg 2000w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/iPhone-Comp-Template.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/iPhone-Comp-Template.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/iPhone-Comp-Template.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/iPhone-Comp-Template.jpg?resize=680,453 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/iPhone-Comp-Template.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/iPhone-Comp-Template.jpg?resize=50,33 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p>Oh, those webcam improvements. Apple says that the ISP in the M1 machines is improved from the previous generations. But the camera itself is the same 720p webcam we&#8217;ve had in MacBooks forever. In my tests the result of that is that the webcam is still bad, it&#8217;s just less bad. Maybe it crosses the line into &#8216;ok&#8217; because of the better white balance and slightly  improved noise handling. It&#8217;s still not great though.</p>\n<p><b>Cooling and throttling</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">No matter how long the tests I ran were, I was never able to ascertain any throttling of the CPU on the M1 MacBook Pro. From our testing it was evident that in longer operations (20-40 minutes on up) it was possible to see the MacBook Air pulling back a bit over time. Not so with the Macbook Pro. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Apple says that it has designed a new ‘cooling system’ in the M1 MacBook Pro, which holds up. There is a single fan but it is </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">noticeably</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> quieter than either of the other fans. In fact, I was never able to get the M1 much hotter than ‘warm’ and the fan ran at speeds that were much more similar to that of a water cooled rig than the turbo engine situation in the other MacBooks. </span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Cinebench-R23.png\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2074986\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Cinebench-R23.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"631\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Cinebench-R23.png 1876w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Cinebench-R23.png?resize=150,92 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Cinebench-R23.png?resize=300,185 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Cinebench-R23.png?resize=768,473 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Cinebench-R23.png?resize=680,419 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Cinebench-R23.png?resize=1536,946 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Cinebench-R23.png?resize=50,31 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Even running a long, intense Cinebench 23 session could not make the M1 MacBook get loud. Over the course of the mark running all high-performance cores regularly hit 3GHz and the efficiency cores hitting 2GHz. Despite that, it continued to run </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">very </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">cool and </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">very</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> quiet in comparison to other MacBooks. It’s the stealth bomber at the Harrier party.</span></p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet\" data-width=\"550\" data-dnt=\"true\">\n<p lang=\"en\" dir=\"ltr\">I noticed a lot of reviews getting confused by the CPU clock and what the specs were there, here&#39;s a live dump of what the Performance and Efficiency clusters are up to during a Cinebench R23 multithread bench. <a href=\"https://t.co/aixPpebI78\">pic.twitter.com/aixPpebI78</a></p>\n<p>&#8212; First was a 5 minute 4k60 timeline shot with iPhone 12 Pro using audio, transitions, titles and color grading. The M1 Macbook performed fantastic, slightly beating out the 16” MacBook Pro. </span></p>\n<p>&#160;</p>\n<p>&#160;</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export.png\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2074989\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"633\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export.png 1866w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export.png?resize=150,93 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export.png?resize=300,186 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export.png?resize=768,475 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export.png?resize=680,421 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export.png?resize=1536,950 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export.png?resize=50,31 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">With an 8K timeline of the same duration, the 16” MacBook Pro with its Radeon 5500M was able to really shine with FCP’s GPU acceleration. The M1 held its own though, showing 3x faster speeds than the 13” MacBook Pro with its integrated graphics. </span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export-Battery-Remaining.png\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2074988\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export-Battery-Remaining.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"633\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export-Battery-Remaining.png 1866w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export-Battery-Remaining.png?resize=150,93 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export-Battery-Remaining.png?resize=300,186 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export-Battery-Remaining.png?resize=768,475 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export-Battery-Remaining.png?resize=680,421 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export-Battery-Remaining.png?resize=1536,950 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Final-Cut-Pro-5-minute-8K-Timeline-Export-Battery-Remaining.png?resize=50,31 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p>&#160;</p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">And, most impressively, the M1 MacBook Pro used extremely little power to do so. Just 17% of the battery to output an 81GB 8k render. The 13” MacBook Pro could not even finish this render on one battery charge. </span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal.png\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2074993\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"630\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal.png 1876w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal.png?resize=150,92 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal.png?resize=300,185 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal.png?resize=768,472 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal.png?resize=680,418 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal.png?resize=1536,945 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal.png?resize=50,31 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal-Offscreen.png\"><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout aligncenter size-full wp-image-2074992\" https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal-Offscreen.png?resize=300,185 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal-Offscreen.png?resize=768,473 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal-Offscreen.png?resize=680,419 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal-Offscreen.png?resize=1536,947 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/GFXBench-Metal-Offscreen.png?resize=50,31 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<p>As you can see in these GFXBench charts, while the M1 MacBook Pro isn&#8217;t a powerhouse gaming laptop we still got some <em>very </em>surprising and impressive results in tests of the GPU when a rack of Metal tests were run on it. The 16&#8243; MBP still has more raw power, but rendering games at retina is still very possible here.</p>\n<p><b>The M1 is the future of CPU design</b></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">All too often over the years we’ve seen Mac releases hamstrung by the capabilities of the chips and chipsets that were being offered by Intel. Even as recently as the 16” MacBook Pro, Apple was stuck a generation or more behind. The writing was basically on the wall once the iPhone became such a massive hit that Apple began producing more chips than the entire rest of the computing industry combined. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Apple has now shipped over 2 billion chips, a scale that makes Intel’s desktop business look like a luxury manufacturer. I think it was politic of Apple to not </span><a href=\"https://twitter.com/panzer/status/1326225964989259776?s=20\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">mention them by name</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> during last week’s announcement, but it’s also clear that Intel’s days are numbered on the Mac and that their only saving grace for the rest of the industry is that Apple is incredibly unlikely to make chips for anyone else.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Years ago I wrote an article about the </span><a href=\"https://thenextweb.com/apple/2013/03/09/the-iphone-battery-intervention/\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">iPhone’s biggest flaw</span></a><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> being that its performance per watt limited the new experiences that it was capable of delivering. People hated that piece but I was right. Apple has spent the last decade “fixing” its battery problem by continuing to carve out massive performance gains via its A-series chips all while maintaining essentially the same (or slightly better) battery life across the iPhone lineup. No miracle battery technology has appeared, so Apple went in the opposite direction, grinding away at the chip end of the stick.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">What we’re seeing today is the result of Apple flipping the switch to bring all of that power efficiency to the Mac, a device with 5x the raw battery to work with. And those results are spectacular.</span></p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"gEz7XZx6dD\"><p><a Mac</a></p></blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" title=\"&#8220;The new Mac mini: The revival of the no-compromise low-cost 00:31:14","updatedTime":"2020-11-18 00:31:14","title":"Levels raises $12M from a16z and others to bring its biowearable to market","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2074773","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\"><a href=\"https://www.levelshealth.com\">Levels</a> today is announcing a large seed round to help bring its biowearable metabolic sensor to market. The innovative platform pairs continuous glucose monitoring (CGM) with an impressive software suite to provide the wearer with deep insights about their health. The company&#8217;s founders strongly feel that this approach helps users close the loop between food, exercise and well-being.</p>\n<p>Levels provides actionable health information by constantly monitoring the wearer&#8217;s blood sugar using proven technology. This isn&#8217;t a wearable worn around the wrist; Levels uses medical hardware that&#8217;s attached to the wearer&#8217;s arm for two weeks. It sounds scarier than it is. I have one on right now, and it&#8217;s painless.</p>\n<p>The $12 million seed round was led by Andreessen Horowitz with other notable investors participating, including Marc Randolph (co-founder and first CEO of Netflix), Dick Costolo (former CEO of Twitter), Michael Arrington (founder of TechCrunch), and Matt Dellavedova (NBA, Cleveland Cavaliers).</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>Levels says there are 45,000 people on its waitlist, and with this funding, the company expects to fulfill all pre-orders and millions more. The company expects customers will be able to purchase Levels starting in early 2021.</p>\n<p>&#8220;Levels is ultimately a behavior change mechanism,&#8221; CEO and co-founder Josh Clemente said. &#8220;We will continue to establish product development milestones that are focused on achieving that end state before we fully launch. This funding will allow us to grow the team and perform the research necessary to understand specifically which mechanisms to focus on and translate those into the product. Once we reach that degree of efficacy, we&#8217;ll launch.&#8221;</p>\n<p>The founders of Level are passionate about this cause. Over several phone calls with TechCrunch, Levels&#8217; founders Josh Clemente and Dr. Casey Means explained the company&#8217;s targets.</p>\n<p>&#8220;Optimizing metabolic function can improve energy, endurance, memory, mood, and cognitive performance. Seven of the ten leading causes of death in the US are strongly related to metabolic dysfunction,&#8221; said Dr. Casey Means, Co-founder of Levels. &#8220;Levels helps you improve metabolic fitness by alerting you to foods that negatively impact glucose levels. Armed with this information, you can take control of your metabolic health and make healthier lifestyle decisions.&#8221;</p>\n<p><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-2074786\" serious about its own branded headphones, Beats have, perhaps, lost a little bit of luster within the company. The products are still wildly popular, of course, and the brand offers a lot more options than its parent.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\">Powerbeats are one of the more utilitarian entries among the brand’s wireless offerings (though not quite to the extent of those <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/10/13/beats-announces-50-bluetooth-earbuds/\">new $50 models</a>), trading the fully wireless form factor for a behind-the-neck cable and a lower price. 00:31:14","updatedTime":"2020-11-18 00:31:14","title":"Mati reshapes online trust and reputation with a Plaid-like API","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2075069","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Meet <a href=\"https://www.getmati.com\">Mati</a>, a startup that recently raised a $13.5 million Series A round to build a digital reputation API that could change the way you interact with online services. <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/mati\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"mati\">Mati <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> uses an API-first approach and lets users seamlessly share pieces of their legal identity.</p>\n<p>Investors in today’s funding round include Tribe Capital with Arjun Sethi joining the board, Jerry Murdock from Insight Partners, Sima Gandhi who is the former head of business development and strategy at Plaid and Will Hockey, the CTO of Plaid.</p>\n<p>Mati isn’t just an ID verification company with biometric checks. In many ways, Mati works a bit like Plaid, but not just for other types of data. When a company starts using Mati, they can verify various data points, such as residency, income and taxes.</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>Instead of taking a photography of important documents, Mati can help you connect to a government database or a utility provider to download and share data from those services directly.</p>\n<p>“What we do is a digital reputation API that turns anonymous strangers into trustworthy users,” co-founder and CEO Filip Victor told me. And this kind of verifications are extremely important for fintech startups and companies operating in the so-called sharing economy industry.</p>\n<p>Filip Victor himself has suffered in the past from poor reputation processes. As an immigrant in the U.S., he couldn’t verify himself on Airbnb. The issue is that most reputation services aren’t that flexible. You run into edge cases quite quickly when you’re an immigrant or you don’t have the right documents.</p>\n<p>Mati has chosen to focus on high-growth regions, such as Latin America and South East Asia because they tend to be underserved when it comes to digital reputation. Clients include Te Creemos, Taptap Send and Tropipay.</p>\n<p>“We went region by region, country by country and scraped into different databases. We gained access to things like social security access via the user,” Victor said.</p>\n<p>Mati is thinking about other use cases beyond financial services and sharing economy startups. Many online services could benefit from some level of trust. By letting users choose what they want and don’t want to share, the company believes it has found the right balance between privacy and trust.</p>\n<p>“Privacy is not about being anonymous or hidden. It’s about controlling your data,” Victor said.</p>\n<p>For now, the company has managed to attract 200 customers. In the past year, Mati has tripled the number of customers. The startup now has 50 employees and plans to add another 20 employees with the Series A round.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2075071\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><img Initially, Instagram limited Guides to a select group of creators who were publishing content focused on mental health and well-being. Today, the company says it&#8217;s making the format available to all users, and expanding Guides to include other types of content, as well &#8212; including Products, Places, and Posts.</p>\n<p>TechCrunch<a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/08/26/instagram-guides-may-soon-allow-creators-to-recommended-places-products-and-more/\"> in August noted an expansion</a> of Instagram Guides appeared to be in development, with a focus on allowing users to create travel guides and product recommendation guides, in addition to a more generic &#8220;posts&#8221; format.</p>\n<p>This &#8220;Guides&#8221; format was designed to give Instagram creators and marketers a way to share long-form content on a social network that had been, until now, focused more on media &#8212; like photos and videos. By comparison, an Instagram Guide could look more like a blog post, as it could include text accompanied by photos, galleries and videos to illustrate the subject matter being discussed.</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>The feature could help increase users&#8217; time in the app, since users wouldn&#8217;t have to click through to external websites and blogs to access these posts &#8212; for instance, through a link in the creator&#8217;s bio or through a link added to one of the creator&#8217;s Stories.</p>\n<p>With the expansion to Products, Places and Posts, Instagram&#8217;s Guides can now cover more areas. Instagram says it made the feature easier to use, too. It may also feature Product Guides inside its new shopping destination on the platform, Instagram Shop, the company noted.</p>\n<p>Visitors to Guides can share the Guides across their own Stories and data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"google\">Google <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> today announced an update to Google Maps that includes a number of new COVID-related features, as well as the ability to see the live status of your takeout or delivery orders, as well as the launch of the long-expected new Assistant driving mode.</p>\n<p>In addition, the company shared a few new stats around Google Maps today. The company says that it makes 50 million updates to Maps each day now, for example, though that includes user-generated content like user reviews, photos and ratings. The company also now features &#8220;popular times&#8221; information for 20 million places around the globe.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2074740\" style=\"width: 690px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-2.png\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2074740\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"wp-image-2074740 size-large\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-2.png?w=680\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"425\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-2.png 1600w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-2.png?resize=150,94 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-2.png?resize=300,188 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-2.png?resize=768,480 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-2.png?resize=680,425 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-2.png?resize=1536,960 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-2.png?resize=50,31 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2074740\" class=\"wp-caption-text\">Image Credits: Google Maps</p></div></p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>As far as COVID is concerned, there are two announcements here. First, Google is updating the COVID layer in Google Maps on <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/android\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"android\">Android <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> and iOS with some new information, including the number of all-time detected cases in an area and links to COVID resources from local governments. <span style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\">Second, Google Maps can now tell you, in real time, how busy a given transit line is so you can avoid packed trains or busses, for example. That&#8217;s based on real-time feedback from Google Maps users and will feel familiar if you are aware of how Google Maps can already show you how busy a given store or restaurant currently is.</span></p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2074732\" style=\"width: 690px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.jpeg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2074732\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"wp-image-2074732 size-large\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.jpeg?w=680\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"680\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.jpeg 1000w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.jpeg?resize=150,150 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.jpeg?resize=300,300 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.jpeg?resize=768,768 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.jpeg?resize=680,680 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.jpeg?resize=32,32 32w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.jpeg?resize=50,50 50w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.jpeg?resize=64,64 64w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.jpeg?resize=96,96 96w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.jpeg?resize=128,128 128w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2074732\" class=\"wp-caption-text\">Image Credits: Google Maps</p></div></p>\n<p>Semi-related &#8212; delivery services are booming during the pandemic, after all (even as they continue to struggle to make a <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=tO5sxLapAts\">profit</a>) &#8212; Google Maps on mobile will now be able to show you the live delivery status of your takeout and delivery orders in the U.S., Canada, Germany, Australia, Brazil and India. To do so, you have to book your order from Google Maps on Android or iOS.</p>\n<p>For Google Maps users who don&#8217;t have an Android Auto-compatible car, the new Google Assistant driving mode in Maps has long been something to look forward to. The company first <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2018/05/08/google-assistant-is-coming-to-google-maps/\">talked</a> about this set of new features at its I/O developers conference <a href=\"https://blog.google/products/assistant/next-generation-google-assistant-io/\">in May 2019</a>, but as is so often the case, features announced at I/O take a while to get to market. Originally, this was supposed to launch last summer.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2074741\" style=\"width: 690px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.gif\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2074741\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"wp-image-2074741 size-large\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/image-1.gif?w=680\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"574\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2074741\" class=\"wp-caption-text\">Image Credits: Google Maps</p></div></p>\n<p>The idea here is to allow drivers to get alerts about incoming calls, have the Assistant read out text messages and control your music right inside of Google Maps. Using the Assistant ideally reduces driver distractions. For now, this new mode is only coming to Android users in the U.S., though, and the number of features it supports remains limited. Google promises to support more features over time, but it&#8217;s not clear which features it plans to add to this mode.</p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"GCXfl9r5P2\"><p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2018/05/08/google-assistant-is-coming-to-google-maps/\">Google Assistant is coming to Google Maps</a></p></blockquote>\n<p><iframe 00:31:14","updatedTime":"2020-11-18 00:31:14","title":"Facebook’s Messenger Kids app redesigned to look more like Messenger","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2074791","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\"><a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/facebook\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"facebook\">Facebook <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> today is <a href=\"https://messengernews.fb.com/2020/11/17/messenger-kids-tests-new-design/\">rolling out an updated version</a> of its Messenger Kids app with the goal of making it easier for kids to interact with their friends and family, navigate the app, and personalize their experience with features like custom chat bubble colors. The redesign also gives the kid-friendly app a look-and-feel that&#8217;s more like <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/messenger\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"messenger\">Messenger <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> itself.</p>\n<p>The updated app does away with the larger, colorful blocks that would flash when messages arrive for a more traditional messaging app design where chats are stacked in a vertical list. The child&#8217;s unread messages, now at the top of the inbox, are in bold with a blue dot next to them to call the eye&#8217;s attention. Media and message previews have also been added, too, allowing kids to more easily see updates for their conversations.</p>\n<p>The redesign introduces new navigation with two dedicated &#8220;Chat&#8221; and &#8220;Explore&#8221; navigation tabs at the bottom of the screen, allowing for kids to switch between their conversations and the other in-app activities the app provides, like its mini-games</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>And with a new swipe gesture, kids can start a call from their inbox.</p>\n<p>Finally, the update introduces a new option to personalize conversations, including both individual and group chats, with a custom chat bubble color.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2075075\" style=\"width: 690px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2075075\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"size-large wp-image-2075075\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_4.png?w=680\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"383\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_4.png 1921w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_4.png?resize=150,84 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_4.png?resize=300,169 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_4.png?resize=768,432 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_4.png?resize=680,383 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_4.png?resize=1536,864 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_4.png?resize=50,28 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" /><p id=\"caption-attachment-2075075\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> Facebook</p></div></p>\n<p>Facebook refers to the update as a &#8220;test,&#8221; but the changes here are not small tweaks to the layout, navigation or feature set &#8212; they&#8217;re a revamp. That makes it less likely that this is just some experiment that will later be rolled back based on user feedback. Instead, by referring to it as a test, Facebook gives itself more time before committing to a global rollout.</p>\n<p>The company says the new features will first roll out to kids using iPhones in the U.S. and Canada. The update will later expand to other devices and markets in the months ahead.</p>\n<p>The changes arrive shortly after Messenger itself <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/10/13/messengers-latest-update-brings-new-features-cross-app-communication-with-instagram/\">received a significant update of its own</a>, which included a visual makeover and new features, including support for chat themes, custom reactions, selfie stickers and vanish mode, in addition to support for cross-app communication with Instagram users. Those updates could have led to the Messenger Kids makeover as well, given there&#8217;s likely some underlying messaging infrastructure that&#8217;s shared here.</p>\n<p>The Messenger Kids app has been steadily updated in the years since its launch, most recently <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/02/04/messenger-kids-adds-expanded-parental-controls-details-how-much-kids-data-facebook-collects/\">with a big explainer on what Facebook is doing with all that data it&#8217;s collecting</a>.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2075078\" style=\"width: 690px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2075078\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"size-large wp-image-2075078\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_3.png?w=680\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"383\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_3.png 1920w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_3.png?resize=150,84 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_3.png?resize=300,169 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_3.png?resize=768,432 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_3.png?resize=680,383 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_3.png?resize=1536,865 1536w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/Messenger-Kids-Design-Update_3.png?resize=50,28 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" /><p id=\"caption-attachment-2075078\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> Facebook</p></div></p>\n<p>Parents should be aware this app today collects a lot of personal information, including names, profile photos, demographic details (gender and birthday), a child’s connection to parents, contacts’ information (like most frequent contacts), app usage information, device attributes and unique identifiers, data from device settings (like time zones or access to camera and photos), network information and information provided from things like bug reports or feedback/contact forms. While some of this does allow the app to properly function, there&#8217;s also concern from some parents about how this data is really being used.</p>\n<p>While the app does offer a suite of parental controls that make it easier for parents to monitor and restrict how and when their children chat online, Messenger Kids&#8217; privacy policy still leaves itself a lot of wiggle room about how the data may be used to &#8220;evaluate, troubleshoot, improve, create, and develop our products” and be shared with other <a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/help/111814505650678?ref=dp\">Facebook Companies</a>. Parents should carefully weigh the risks of allowing their child to use a Facebook product with the conveniences of being able to use an app with a robust set of parental controls.</p>\n<div
